import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    1D depthwise separable convolution block: pointwise Conv1d (channel mixing), depthwise Conv1d (spatial),
    GELU activation, and residual addition. Used in genomic foundation models (Enformer) for efficient
    sequence processing of DNA/RNA.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(channels, expanded_channels, 1)
        self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size,
                                   padding=kernel_size // 2, groups=expanded_channels)
        self.activation = nn.GELU()
        self.project_back = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, channels, 1)

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        x = self.pointwise(x)
        x = self.depthwise(x)
        x = self.activation(x)
        x = self.project_back(x)
        x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
expanded_channels = 512
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 9

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size]

GELU activation 4. pointwise Conv1d back to channels 5. residual addition Input shape: (batch_size, channels, seq_len) After pointwise1: (batch_size, expanded_channels, seq_len) After depthwise: same shape (padding same) After GELU: same shape After pointwise2: (batch_size, channels, seq_len) Then add residual. We need to write a fused CUDA kernel. We need to consider gpu specs to tune block sizes, occupancy, memory bandwidth. Let's get GPU specs first. Potential strategy: fuse entire block into one kernel per batch element, or per sample. Because channels=256, expanded=512, seq_len=4096, batch=32, kernel_size=9. This is fairly large. compute amount: - pointwise1: batch * seq_len * channels * expanded_channels = 32 * 4096 * 256 * 512 = ~17.1 billion multiply-adds? Wait: 32*4096=131072, *256=33,554,432, *512 = 17,179,869,184. Yes ~17.2B FLOPs. - depthwise: batch * expanded_channels * seq_len * kernel_size = 32*512*4096*9 = ~603M FLOPs. - GELU: batch * expanded_channels * seq_len ~ 67M. - pointwise2: batch * seq_len * expanded_channels * channels = another ~17.2B. Total ~35B FLOPs. That's a lot. Memory: input 32*256*4096*4 = 134 MB. Intermediate after pointwise1: 32*512*4096*4 = 268 MB. After depthwise: same. After pointwise2: 134 MB. Actually compute-bound maybe. The GPU may be Ampere or similar. Let's get specs first. We need to write efficient kernel. Since seq_len=4096 and channels=256, expanded=512, this is basically a small GEMM (channels x expanded) applied per position, i.e., two 1x1 convs. We can treat pointwise1 as: for each (b, t), compute y[b,t,:] = W1 @ x[b,t,:] + b1. That's a matrix-vector multiplication. Since seq_len is large, we can parallelize over b and t. Similarly pointwise2 is y2 = W2 @ x + b2. Depthwise is a 1D convolution over t per channel. We can fuse pointwise1 + depthwise + GELU + pointwise2 + residual into one kernel. For each output position, we need to load a window of the input across channels to compute pointwise1, then compute depthwise across expanded_channels, apply GELU, then pointwise2 to channels, then add residual. However doing this per output position would require loading kernel_size windows of the intermediate from pointwise1, which might be expensive but okay if kept in registers/shared memory? But intermediate expanded_channels=512, kernel_size=9. If we compute per output position independently, each thread loads 512*9 = 4608 floats for the depthwise alone, too much. Better approach: tile over channels and sequence. Alternative: implement pointwise1 as a batched GEMM (B, T) x (C, E) -> (B, T, E). Then depthwise as a separate kernel over (B, E, T) with sliding window. Then GELU+pointwise2+residual fused. Because pointwise1 and pointwise2 are both GEMMs, we can leverage cuBLAS? But we need custom kernel to replace forward. Could call torch.matmul? That would use PyTorch's optimized kernels but we need a custom CUDA kernel. The task is to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward. We can define multiple CUDA functions and call them sequentially. But maybe we can write a single kernel that fuses everything using shared memory tiling. Let's get GPU specs to know memory bandwidth and compute.

GPU is H100 with 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth and 67 TFLOPS FP32. This is a memory-bound problem since we're doing convolutions with large spatial dimension and relatively small kernels. The main operations are: - Pointwise conv: effectively matrix multiplications - Depthwise conv: sliding window per channel - GELU: elementwise - Residual add: elementwise For a fused kernel, the best approach is to: 1. Fuse all operations into a single kernel 2. Process tiles of the sequence to maximize shared memory reuse 3. For the depthwise conv with kernel_size=9, we can load a tile into shared memory and apply the stencil Let me think about the fused approach: - For each output position, we need: - The residual (for final add) - The input to compute pointwise outputs - For each expanded channel output of pointwise, we need depthwise conv with kernel_size=9, then GELU, then project back Actually, let's think more carefully. The operations are: 1. y_pointwise[b, e, i] = sum_c W_pw[e, c] * x[b, c, i] 2. y_depthwise[b, e, i] = sum_k W_dw[e, k] * y_pointwise[b, e, i + k - padding] 3. y_activated[b, e, i] = GELU(y_depthwise[b, e, i]) 4. y_out[b, c, i] = sum_e W_pb[c, e] * y_activated[b, e, i] 5. out[b, c, i] = y_out[b, c, i] + x[b, c, i] To fuse this efficiently, we need to process tiles. For each output channel c and position i: - Compute all expanded channel values at position i (pointwise) - For each expanded channel, gather the depthwise neighborhood - Apply depthwise + GELU + project back - Add residual But the depthwise conv needs a neighborhood of 9 positions for each expanded channel. So for a tile of positions, we need the pointwise outputs for a slightly larger tile. Let me design a kernel: - Block dimensions: (block_channels, block_seq) - Each thread computes partial outputs for some (b, c, i) - Actually, we could have each thread handle one (b, c) and a strip of i, or one (b, i) and handle channels. Given H100 has high memory bandwidth but this is memory-bound, fusion should help a lot by reducing memory traffic. Let me think about tiling: - We have batch=32, channels=256, seq_len=4096 - If we use block size like (16, 16) in (c, i) with batch handled by grid - Or we can have each block handle a tile of (batch, channels, seq) For the depthwise with kernel_size=9, for each position i, the depthwise convolution for expanded channel e needs y_pw[b, e, i-4:i+5]. A key optimization: instead of storing intermediate pointwise outputs in global memory, compute them on the fly and keep them in registers/shared memory for the depthwise. But for the depthwise, each output position i for expanded channel e requires 9 pointwise values. If we process a tile of positions [i_start, i_start+T) for all expanded channels, we need pointwise values for [i_start-4, i_start+T+4). For expanded_channels=512, that's a lot of data. For tile size T=64 in sequence, we'd need 512 * 72 = 36864 floats = 147KB in shared memory. That's a lot but manageable. Actually, let me reconsider. For each output channel c, we need: out[b, c, i] = x[b, c, i] + sum_e W_pb[c, e] * GELU(sum_k W_dw[e, k] * sum_{c'} W_pw[e, c'] * x[b, c', i+k-pad]) This is a very wide computation. To avoid excessive shared memory, we can process in tiles. Alternative approach: Use CUTLASS/implicit gemm style for 1D conv, or use im2col + matmul for depthwise. Actually, for channels=256, expanded=512, seq_len=4096, batch=32: - Pointwise 1: 256 * 512 * 32 * 4096 = 17.2B multiply-adds - Depthwise: 512 * 9 * 32 * 4096 = 603M multiply-adds - Pointwise 2: 512 * 256 * 32 * 4096 = 17.2B multiply-adds - Total: ~35B operations At 67 TFLOPS, this is about 0.5ms compute-bound. For the fused kernel, I think the most practical approach is: 1. Load a tile of input x into shared memory (or keep in registers) 2. Let me consider a different tiling strategy: - Process sequence in tiles of, say, 64 or 128 - Each thread block handles: all batches × a subset of output channels × a tile of sequence - Or: a subset of batches × all output channels × a tile of sequence Option A: Thread block = (block_c, block_i), each handling one batch - Grid = (batch, channels/block_c, seq_len/block_i) - For each position i in the tile and each output channel c: - Load x[:, c', i] for all input channels c' into registers/shared memory - Compute y_pw[e, i] for all e (512 values) - this is a matvec or small GEMV - Then apply depthwise to y_pw[e, :] for each e - Then GELU + project back The issue is that computing all 512 expanded values per position requires 256*512 = 131K multiplies. That's too much per thread. Let me try a simpler approach that should work: **Simple but effective approach:** Process all operations but tile at the block level. Each block handles one batch element and a contiguous chunk of sequence positions. Within the block, all 256 threads cooperate. Pseudo-code for the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv_kernel( float* out, const float* x, const float* W_pw, const float* W_dw, const float* W_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int seq_start = blockIdx.y * SEQ_TILE; // Load x for this batch and seq tile + halo into shared memory // x_shared[C][SEQ_TILE + K - 1] for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < C * (SEQ_TILE + K - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (SEQ_TILE + K - 1); int s = idx % (SEQ_TILE + K - 1); int global_s = seq_start + s - K/2; if (global_s >= 0 && global_s < L) x_shared[c][s] = x[b * C * L + c * L + global_s]; else x_shared[c][s] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread handles some (c_out, s) pairs int num_outputs = C * SEQ_TILE; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < num_outputs; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / SEQ_TILE; int s = idx % SEQ_TILE; float sum = 0.0f; for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { // depthwise for this e and output position s float dw = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { // pointwise for this e and position s+k-K/2 float pw = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; c_in++) { pw += W_pw[e * C + c_in] * x_shared[c_in][s + k]; } dw += W_dw[e * K + k] * pw; } float act = gelu(dw); sum += W_pb[c * E + e] * act; } float residual = x_shared[c][s + K/2]; out[b * C * L + c * L + seq_start + s] = sum + residual; } } ``` Wait, this has a critical problem: the innermost loop computes the pointwise from x_shared for every (e, k) combination. For each output (c, s), we do 512 * 9 * 256 = 1.18M loads from x_shared. With 256 threads / block and 32 * 64 = 2048 outputs per block, total work is 2048 * 1.18M = 2.4B operations per block. That's way too much. The right way to do this is: 1. Precompute Y_pw for all (e, pos) and store in shared memory or registers 2. Then do depthwise on Y_pw 3. Then do output projection But Y_pw[512][72] = 36864 floats = ~147KB. Plus x_shared[256][72] = ~72KB. Total ~220KB. H100 has 228KB shared memory per SM. With some shared memory used for other things, this might just fit! Actually, if we use shared memory just for x and Y_pw: - x_shared: 256 * 72 * 4 = 73,728 bytes - y_pw_shared: 512 * 72 * 4 = 147,456 bytes - Total: 221,184 bytes H100 max shared memory per block is 227KB. So this is extremely tight! And we also need space for weights. Alternatively, we can store Y_pw in registers across threads. 36864 floats / 256 threads = 144 floats per thread. That's a lot but manageable if we don't have too many other registers. Wait, 144 floats = 576 bytes per thread. At 256 threads, total registers = 144K floats = 576KB. H100 has 64K registers per SM, with 256 threads that's 256 registers per thread max. 144 floats of storage is too much if we need them all live at once. Hmm, but we don't need all 72 positions per thread. We can process in smaller groups. Here's an improved approach: - Process expanded channels in tiles of, say, 64 - For each expanded tile: - Load W_pw for this tile into shared memory - Compute Y_pw[64][72] into shared memory - Apply depthwise + GELU on Y_pw[64][72] -> Y_act[64][64] (for valid output positions) - Accumulate into output registers using W_pb - Reuse the shared memory for the next tile For this approach, per block: - x_shared: 256 * 72 * 4 = 73KB - W_pw_shared: 64 * 256 * 4 = 64KB - Y_pw_shared: 64 * 72 * 4 = 18KB - W_pb_shared: 64 * 64 * 4 = 16KB (for output channel tile) - Total: ~171KB per block - more manageable! Wait, but we need to accumulate the output projection across all expanded tiles. Each thread can hold its output values in registers. If each thread handles, say, 4 output channels × 16 sequence positions = 64 outputs in registers = 256 bytes. That's fine. Let me refine: Block structure: - 256 threads - Each block handles: 1 batch, all 256 output channels? No, too many. - Each block handles: 1 batch, output_channel_tile (say 64 channels), seq_tile (say 64 positions) Grid: (batch, 256/64=4, 4096/64=64) For each block: - Initialize output registers for 64 channels × 64 positions to 0 - For expanded_tile in [0, 64, 128, ..., 448]: - Load x_shared[256][72] - Load W_pw_shared[64][256] for this expanded tile - Compute Y_pw[64][72] using all threads - Apply depthwise + GELU per thread for assigned positions - Load W_pb_shared[64][64] for current out_channel_tile and expanded_tile - Accumulate output - Add residual and write back Computing Y_pw[64][72]: - 4608 output values - Each is dot product of 256 elements - Total 4608 * 256 = 1.18M FMAs - With 256 threads, ~4608 FMAs each = ~2300 cycles at 2GHz = ~1.15us - This is very feasible! Let me also think about memory coalescing. Loading x: - x is stored as [batch, channel, seq] - For contiguous sequence positions, consecutive threads should access consecutive memory - Thread idx accesses x_shared[c][s] = x[b][c][global_s] - So for fixed c, threads with consecutive idx (and hence consecutive s) access consecutive global memory. Good. - But x_shared layout: we want threads accessing x_shared with different c to not conflict. For W_pw: [expanded][in_channel]. Thread t loads W_pw[e][c] for some e, c. If we load sequentially with striding, we might not get perfect coalescing unless multiple threads load the same e and consecutive c. Let me structure the weight loading: ```cuda // Load W_pw[64][256] into shared memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * 256; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx / 256; int c = idx % 256; W_pw_shared[idx] = W_pw[(expanded_tile + e) * C + c]; } ``` Consecutive threads load consecutive elements of W_pw. Let me think about kernel 2 in detail: - Y_act: [32, 512, 4096] - W_pb: [256, 512] - x: [32, 256, 4096] - output: [32, 256, 4096] For one batch element and sequence tile of 64: - Y_act_local: [512, 64] - output_local: [256, 64] = W_pb[256, 512] @ Y_act[512, 64] - Then add x[b, :, seq_start:seq_start+64] We can implement this as a tiled GEMM. Block handles, say, 64 output channels × 64 sequence positions. Wait, but 64 channels × 64 positions = 4096 outputs. With 256 threads, 16 outputs per thread. Standard approach for small GEMM: - Block tile: 64×64 output - Each block handles this tile - Threads cooperate to compute the matrix multiplication - We load W_pb tiles and Y_act tiles into shared memory For example, using 256 threads to compute a 64×64 tile of C = A @ B where A is 256×512 and B is 512×64: - Block tile of C: 64 × 64 - A tile: 64 × K (where K is tile size, say 64) - B tile: K × 64 = 64 × 64 - These fit in shared memory: 64*64 + 64*64 + 64*64 = 12K floats = 48KB. Very small. - Grid: 32 batch × 4 channel tiles (256/64) × 64 seq tiles = 8192 blocks. - Each block is pure compute with good coalescing. This is very efficient! And it's only one extra kernel launch. For kernel 1 (pointwise + depthwise + GELU): - Block handles 64 expanded channels × 64 sequence positions - 256 threads - Grid: 32 batch × 8 expanded tiles × 64 seq tiles = 16384 blocks - No output channel dimension, so no redundancy - Memory: read x (with halo), write Y_act For kernel 1, we also need to write Y_act. Y_act is [batch, 512, 4096]. For a block handling 64 expanded channels and 64 positions, writing is: each thread writes some (e, s) pairs to Y_act. Coalescing for Y_act: consecutive sequence positions in the same channel are consecutive in memory. So threads writing the same channel but different positions are coalesced. If we use the warp approach where each warp handles one expanded channel: - 2 warps per channel? For 64 positions, 1 warp handles 32 positions, 2 warps handle 64 positions. - 64 expanded channels would need 128 warps = 4096 threads. Too many. Alternative: 256 threads handling 64 channels × 64 positions. 16384 outputs / 256 threads = 64 outputs per thread. Thread assignment: e = threadIdx.x / 4, s_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 16 Each thread handles 1 channel and 16 positions. Then for writing Y_act[e, s_base + j]: - Thread t writes positions s_base to s_base+15 for channel e. - Threads 0..3 write channel 0, positions 0-15, 16-31, 32-47, 48-63. - In a warp (32 threads): 8 channels, 4 threads each. - Threads 0-3: channel 0, positions 0-15, 16-31, 32-47, 48-63 - Threads 4-7: channel 1, positions 0-15, ... - Within a warp, different channels. Scattered writes. But for Y_act, the memory layout is [batch, channel, seq]. Writing different channels at the same position isn't coalesced. Actually, within a single thread's execution, it writes 16 positions for one channel sequentially. If we do float4 writes, that's 4 instructions, each writing 4 consecutive floats. This is 100% coalesced for the thread, but since other threads in the warp write different channels, the overall warp still issues multiple memory transactions. However, since each thread writes sequentially (4 float4 per channel), and the next thread writes a different channel, the warp has 8 channels × 4 float4 = 32 memory operations. These might overlap in flight. With compute being the bottleneck, this could be fine. Actually, let's quantify: Y_act is 268MB. Writing it at full bandwidth takes 80us. Even if write efficiency is 50%, it's 160us. For a kernel doing ~18B FMAs (kernel 1), at peak that's ~540us. So write overhead is maybe 25%. Acceptable. Also, reading Y_act in kernel 2 is 268MB = 80us. Kernel 2 does ~17B FMAs, ~510us at peak. Total memory: 134 + 268 + 268 + 134 = 804MB = 240us. Total compute: 35B FMAs = 1040us. Theoretical: ~1.28ms. If we achieve 50% compute efficiency: ~2.56ms. PyTorch reference: probably 5-10ms (small GEMMs are not very efficient in cuBLAS, plus overhead). So 2-3x speedup seems plausible. Let me now design kernel 1 and kernel 2. Kernel 1: fused_pointwise_depthwise_gelu ```cuda __global__ void kernel1( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_pw, const float* __restrict__ w_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int e_tile = blockIdx.y * 64; int s_tile = blockIdx.z * 64; // Shared memory __shared__ float x_shared[256 * 72]; // actually C * (64 + K - 1) __shared__ float w_pw_shared[64 * 256]; __shared__ float w_dw_shared[64 * 9]; __shared__ float y_pw_shared[64 * 72]; // Load x with halo int s_start = s_tile - K / 2; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < C * (64 + K - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / (64 + K - 1); int s_local = idx % (64 + K - 1); int s_global = s_start + s_local; if (s_global >= 0 && s_global < L) { x_shared[idx] = x[b * C * L + c * L + s_global]; } else { x_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } // Load W_pw for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * C; idx += blockDim.x) { w_pw_shared[idx] = w_pw[e_tile * C + idx]; } // Load W_dw for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * K; idx += blockDim.x) { w_dw_shared[idx] = w_dw[e_tile * K + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute pointwise // Thread assignment: 256 threads, each handles 18 (e,s) pairs // Better: each thread handles 1 e and 18 contiguous s int e_local = threadIdx.x / 4; int s_local_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 18; float y_pw[18]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) y_pw[j] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float w = w_pw_shared[e_local * C + c]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) { y_pw[j] += w * x_shared[c * 72 + s_local_base + j]; } } // Store Y_pw #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) { y_pw_shared[e_local * 72 + s_local_base + j] = y_pw[j]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute depthwise + GELU // Each thread handles some (e, s) for valid output positions s in [0, 63] // 64 e * 64 s = 4096 elements. 256 threads = 16 per thread. // Assignment: e = threadIdx.x / 4, s_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 16 e_local = threadIdx.x / 4; s_local_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 16; float dw[16]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) dw[j] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { float w = w_dw_shared[e_local * K + k]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) { dw[j] += w * y_pw_shared[e_local * 72 + s_local_base + j + k]; } } // GELU and write to global memory #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) { float val = gelu(dw[j]); int e_global = e_tile + e_local; int s_global = s_tile + s_local_base + j; if (s_global < L) { y_act[b * E * L + e_global * L + s_global] = val; } } } ``` Wait, one issue: after the pointwise computation, we need to store Y_pw_shared. But then for depthwise, threads are reassigned. The new assignment `e_local = threadIdx.x / 4` gives e_local values 0..63. Good. But we need to make sure `e_local` for the new assignment matches the `e_local` from the pointwise assignment, or that all Y_pw values are computed before any thread reads them. The `__syncthreads()` ensures this. Actually, in the pointwise step, each thread computes for e_local = t/4. In the depthwise step, each thread computes for e_local = t/4. So it's the same! But s is different: pointwise uses s_base = (t%4)*18, depthwise uses s_base = (t%4)*16. This is fine because: - In pointwise, threads 0..3 all handle e=0 and cover s=0..71 completely. - In depthwise, threads 0..3 handle e=0 and cover s=0..63 completely (with 16 positions each). - The data needed for depthwise at e=0, s=0..63 is stored at y_pw_shared[0][0..71], written by threads 0..3. So this works. Kernel 2: output_projection_residual For kernel 2, we want efficient GEMM-like computation with good coalescing. Operation: output = W_pb @ Y_act + x For one batch element b and sequence tile s_tile: - Y_act is [512, 64] - W_pb is [256, 512] - output is [256, 64] - x is [256, 64] We can tile this as: - Block handles 64 channels × 64 sequence positions = 4096 outputs - But 64 channels out of 256. So grid has 4 channel tiles. - Grid: (batch, 4 channel tiles, 64 seq tiles) = 32 * 4 * 64 = 8192 blocks - Block: 256 threads Within block, we compute C_tile[64, 64] = W_pb_tile[64, 512] @ Y_act_tile[512, 64] This is a standard GEMM. We tile the K dimension (512) into tiles of, say, 64. Shared memory: - W_pb_shared: 64 × 64 = 4096 floats = 16KB - Y_act_shared: 64 × 64 = 4096 floats = 16KB - Total: 32KB. Very comfortable. Algorithm for kernel 2 per block (8 channels, 64 positions): 1. Initialize accum[2] per thread to 0 2. For expanded_tile in [0, 64, ..., 448]: a. Load Y_act[expanded_tile:expanded_tile+64][seq_start:seq_start+64] into Y_act_shared[64][64] b. __syncthreads() c. Each warp (channel c): for e = 0 to 63: w_pb = W_pb[c][e_tile + e] (broadcast) accum0 += w_pb * Y_act_shared[e][s0] accum1 += w_pb * Y_act_shared[e][s1] d. __syncthreads() 3. Load residual x[b][c][seq_start + s0], x[b][c][seq_start + s1] 4. Write output For loading Y_act into shared memory: Y_act is [batch, 512, 4096]. Thread 1: e=0, s=16..31. Address: base + 0*4096 + 16..31. Perfect. Thread 2: e=0, s=32..47. Perfect. Thread 3: e=0, s=48..63. Perfect. Thread 4: e=1, s=0..15. Address: base + 1*4096 + 0..15. Actually, float4 loads: ```cuda float4* y_act_ptr = (float4*)(y_act + b * E * L + e * L + seq_start); // But e varies per thread ``` With float4, thread t can do: ```cuda int e = threadIdx.x / 4; int s4 = threadIdx.x % 4; // 0..3, each loads 4 floats = 16 floats total float4 val = ((float4*)(y_act + b * E * L + e * L + seq_start))[s4]; // store to shared memory ``` This loads 4 float4 per thread, with each float4 being 16 bytes and 16-byte aligned (since seq_start is a multiple of 64, and 64 is divisible by 4). For kernel 2 custom implementation: - As designed: 8 channels per block, 64 positions, 256 threads, perfect coalescing. - Grid: 32 batch × 32 channel tiles × 64 seq tiles = 65536 blocks. - Uses shared memory for Y_act tile [64][64] and loops over expanded tiles. Let me write out kernel 2 in detail: ```cuda __global__ void kernel2( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_tile = blockIdx.y * 8; int s_tile = blockIdx.z * 64; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int c = c_tile + warp_id; // output channel handled by this warp if (c >= C) return; int s0 = lane; int s1 = lane + 32; float accum0 = 0.0f; float accum1 = 0.0f; __shared__ float y_act_shared[64 * 64]; // [64 expanded][64 seq] for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < E; e_tile += 64) { // Load Y_act tile into shared memory // Each thread loads some (e, s) pairs from global memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * 64; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx / 64; int s = idx % 64; int global_s = s_tile + s; if (global_s < L) { y_act_shared[idx] = y_act[b * E * L + (e_tile + e) * L + global_s]; } else { y_act_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Each warp accumulates for its channel for (int e = 0; e < 64; e++) { float w = W_pb[c * E + e_tile + e]; accum0 += w * y_act_shared[e * 64 + s0]; accum1 += w * y_act_shared[e * 64 + s1]; } __syncthreads(); } // Load residual float x0 = (s_tile + s0 < L) ? x[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s0] : 0.0f; float x1 = (s_tile + s1 < L) ? x[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s1] : 0.0f; // Write output if (s_tile + s0 < L) { out[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s0] = accum0 + x0; } if (s_tile + s1 < L) { out[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s1] = accum1 + x1; } } ``` This kernel 2 looks very good. Each warp accesses Y_act_shared[e * 64 + s0] and Y_act_shared[e * 64 + s1]. As analyzed: - For fixed e, lane accesses bank lane and bank lane (for s0 and s1). No bank conflict between threads. - The two accesses from the same thread are sequential, not simultaneous. For loading Y_act: consecutive threads load consecutive elements when e is fixed and s varies. Since idx increments by 1 each time, thread t loads idx, thread t+256 loads idx+256. If blockDim.x = 256, consecutive threads in a warp load consecutive or strided elements. Wait, blockDim.x = 256. Warp has threads 0..31. Thread 0 loads idx=0 (e=0, s=0). Thread 1 loads idx=1 (e=0, s=1). ... Thread 31 loads idx=31 (e=0, s=31). These access memory at addresses base + e*L + s. For e=0 and s=0..31, addresses are base + 0..31. Perfectly coalesced! Thread 32 loads idx=32 (e=0, s=32). Next warp? Threads 32..63 are in warp 1. Warp 1 loads s=32..63 for e=0. Also coalesced. Thread 64 loads idx=64 (e=1, s=0). Warp 2 loads a different e. Still fine. So loading Y_act into shared memory is very efficient. For W_pb access: W_pb[c * E + e_tile + e]. Each warp loads the same W_pb[c][...] values, just different e. For a given e, all threads in the warp load the SAME W_pb value. This is broadcast, very cache efficient. Final writes: each warp writes to channel c, positions s0 and s1. Within a warp, threads write consecutive positions. Coalesced. Final reads for residual: same pattern. Coalesced. This kernel 2 is very efficient! Now kernel 1 needs to write Y_act to global memory. Let's check its write coalescing. Thread assignment for kernel 1 write: - e_local = threadIdx.x / 4 (0..63, but actually we have 64 expanded channels per tile) - s_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 16 - For j = 0..15: write Y_act[b][e_tile + e_local][s_tile + s_base + j] In a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0..3: e=0, s=0..15, 16..31, 32..47, 48..63 - Threads 4..7: e=1, s=0..15, ... - ... For j=0, thread 0 writes to (e=0, s=0). Thread 4 writes to (e=1, s=0). These are 4096 floats apart. Not coalesced within a warp. But each thread writes 16 floats sequentially for the same e. The writes can be float4: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < 16; j += 4) { int e = e_tile + e_local; int s = s_tile + s_base + j; float4 val; val.x = gelu(dw[j]); val.y = gelu(dw[j+1]); // ... ((float4*)(y_act + b * E * L + e * L + s))[0] = val; } ``` Each thread issues 4 float4 writes. These are coalesced for the individual thread. But within a warp, 8 threads (for 8 different e values) write simultaneously to different locations. Since H100 has multiple memory subpartitions, these might execute in parallel. Actually, we could restructure the write pattern in kernel 1 to improve coalescing. What if threads are assigned to write consecutive positions for the SAME expanded channel? For Y_act writing: - We have 64 expanded channels × 64 positions = 4096 outputs. - 256 threads = 16 outputs per thread. - What if: e_local = threadIdx.x / 64? No, 256/64 = 4. e_local = threadIdx.x / 64 gives 0..3. Not enough. What if we use the fact that Y_act is in shared memory after depthwise? We could have a transpose step. Wait, can we fuse the add into torch.matmul using torch.addmm or torch.baddbmm? ```python out = torch.baddbmm(x.unsqueeze(1), self.W_pb.unsqueeze(0), y_act) ``` Where x is [B, C, L], W_pb is [C, E], y_act is [B, E, L]. torch.baddbmm does: out = beta * input + alpha * (batch1 @ batch2) But baddbmm expects 3D tensors: [B, C, E] @ [B, E, L]. Actually, for a compute-bound kernel, the ratio of compute to memory is high, so the memory system should have plenty of time to drain writes. But scattered writes can still cause issues because: 1. They consume more L2 transaction slots 2. They might cause more L2 port contention On H100, each SM has a certain number of store transactions per cycle. If a warp issues 32 store instructions (one per thread), and each goes to a different sector, that's 32 store transactions. At 4 transactions per cycle per SM, that's 8 cycles per warp. For 8 warps, that's a lot. But we have lots of compute between writes. The writes happen once at the end of the kernel, after all compute. So all warps in all blocks will hit the store path at the same time (roughly). This could cause a store storm. But with 2048 blocks and 132 SMs, only ~15.5 blocks per SM. With one block at a time (due to shared memory usage), that's 15.5 sequential store phases per SM. Each store phase needs to write 64KB. At L2 bandwidth of ~12 TB/s, that's 5.3us per block. Total 82us per SM for stores. Since compute is ~1ms, stores are only 8% overhead. Even if uncoalesced stores are 2x slower, it's 16% overhead. Still manageable. That's 32 cache lines. H100 can handle multiple cache lines per warp, but not 32 simultaneously. It might take multiple cycles. However, these accesses are spread across different L2 cache sectors. So within CUDA thread block constraints (max 1024 or 2048 threads), we cannot get perfect coalescing for all 256 channels simultaneously. This means single-kernel will always have compromised write coalescing unless we accept very large blocks (which we can't) or use a different grid structure (which causes redundancy). I'll use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline with two CUDA functions: fused_conv_kernel1 and fused_conv_kernel2. For kernel 1: pointwise + depthwise + GELU For kernel 2: output projection + residual In ModelNew.forward: 1. Call kernel 1: compute y_act from x 2. Call kernel 2: compute output from y_act, W_pb, x, and residual One optimization: can we pre-transpose or reformat weights for better access? For W_pw: [expanded, in_channels]. In kernel 1, we access W_pw[e][c] for fixed e and varying c (in the dot product). So row-major is good. For W_dw: [expanded, kernel_size]. Row-major is fine. For W_pb: [out_channels, expanded]. In kernel 2, we access W_pb[c][e] for fixed c and varying e. Row-major is good. These are already the default PyTorch layouts for conv1d weights. Conv1d weights are [out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size]. For pointwise, it's [expanded, channels, 1]. For depthwise, it's [expanded, 1, kernel_size]. For output projection, it's [channels, expanded, 1]. In PyTorch, conv1d weights are stored as [out_channels, in_channels // groups, kernel_size]. So: - W_pw: [512, 256, 1] -> reshape to [512, 256] - W_dw: [512, 1, 9] -> reshape to [512, 9] - W_pb: [256, 512, 1] -> reshape to [256, 512] We'll need to pass these flattened arrays to CUDA. One more thing: padding. kernel_size = 9, padding = 4. For sequence positions near boundaries (0..3 and 4092..4095), the depthwise conv reads padded zeros. Our kernel handles this by loading 0 for out-of-bounds x positions. Let me now write the actual CUDA code. For kernel 1: ```cuda __global__ void kernel1( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_pw, const float* __restrict__ w_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K, int pad) { int b = blockIdx.x; int e_tile = blockIdx.y * 64; int s_tile = blockIdx.z * 64; // Shared memory __shared__ float x_shared[256 * 72]; __shared__ float w_pw_shared[64 * 256]; __shared__ float w_dw_shared[64 * 9]; __shared__ float y_pw_shared[64 * 72]; const int S = 64; // sequence tile size const int HALO = K - 1; // 8 const int LOAD_S = S + HALO; // 72 // Load x int s_start = s_tile - pad; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < C * LOAD_S; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / LOAD_S; int s_local = idx % LOAD_S; int s_global = s_start + s_local; if (s_global >= 0 && s_global < L) { x_shared[idx] = x[b * C * L + c * L + s_global]; } else { x_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } // Load W_pw for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * C; idx += blockDim.x) { w_pw_shared[idx] = w_pw[e_tile * C + idx]; } // Load W_dw for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * K; idx += blockDim.x) { w_dw_shared[idx] = w_dw[e_tile * K + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute pointwise: Y_pw[64][72] int e_local = threadIdx.x / 4; int s_local_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 18; float y_pw[18]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) { y_pw[j] = 0.0f; } #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float w = w_pw_shared[e_local * C + c]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) { y_pw[j] += w * x_shared[c * LOAD_S + s_local_base + j]; } } #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 18; j++) { y_pw_shared[e_local * LOAD_S + s_local_base + j] = y_pw[j]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute depthwise + GELU: Y_act[64][64] // Assignment: each thread handles 16 (e,s) pairs e_local = threadIdx.x / 4; s_local_base = (threadIdx.x % 4) * 16; float dw[16]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) { dw[j] = 0.0f; } #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { float w = w_dw_shared[e_local * K + k]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) { dw[j] += w * y_pw_shared[e_local * LOAD_S + s_local_base + j + k]; } } // GELU and write #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 16; j++) { float val = 0.5f * dw[j] * (1.0f + erff(dw[j] * 0.70710678118654752440f)); int e_global = e_tile + e_local; int s_global = s_tile + s_local_base + j; if (s_global < L) { y_act[b * E * L + e_global * L + s_global] = val; } } } ``` Wait, I need to handle the case where e_tile + e_local >= E. For our sizes, 512 is divisible by 64, so e_tile goes 0, 64, 128, ..., 448. e_local goes 0..63. So e_global is always valid. For kernel 2: ```cuda __global__ void kernel2( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_tile = blockIdx.y * 8; int s_tile = blockIdx.z * 64; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int c = c_tile + warp_id; if (c >= C) return; int s0 = lane; int s1 = lane + 32; float accum0 = 0.0f; float accum1 = 0.0f; __shared__ float y_act_shared[64 * 64]; for (int e_tile = 0; e_tile < E; e_tile += 64) { // Load Y_act for this tile and sequence range for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * 64; idx += blockDim.x) { int e = idx / 64; int s = idx % 64; int global_s = s_tile + s; if (global_s < L) { y_act_shared[idx] = y_act[b * E * L + (e_tile + e) * L + global_s]; } else { y_act_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate for (int e = 0; e < 64; e++) { float w = w_pb[c * E + e_tile + e]; accum0 += w * y_act_shared[e * 64 + s0]; accum1 += w * y_act_shared[e * 64 + s1]; } __syncthreads(); } // Load residual and write if (s_tile + s0 < L) { float x0 = x[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s0]; out[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s0] = accum0 + x0; } if (s_tile + s1 < L) { float x1 = x[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s1]; out[b * C * L + c * L + s_tile + s1] = accum1 + x1; } } ``` This looks good. So the block-level structure for kernel 1: ``` __global__ void kernel1(y_act, x, W_pw, b_pw, W_dw, b_dw, B, C, E, L, K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int e = blockIdx.y; int l_tile = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int l_start = l_tile * 64; __shared__ float y_pw_shared[64 + 8]; // 72 floats // Compute pointwise values for this tile int num_pw = 64 + (K - 1); // 72 int pw_per_thread = (num_pw + 63) / 64; // 2 for some threads for (int j = 0; j < pw_per_thread; j++) { int pw_idx = tid + j * 64; if (pw_idx < num_pw) { int l = l_start + pw_idx - (K / 2); float dot = 0.0f; if (l >= 0 && l < L) { for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { dot += x[b * C * L + c * L + l] * W_pw[e * C + c]; } dot += b_pw[e]; } else { dot = 0.0f; } y_pw_shared[pw_idx] = dot; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute depthwise + GELU for output int out_l = l_start + tid; if (out_l < L) { float dw = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw += y_pw_shared[tid + k] * W_dw[e * K + k]; } dw += b_dw[e]; y_act[b * E * L + e * L + out_l] = gelu(dw); } } ``` Wait, I need to check: For block (b, e, l_tile) where l_tile goes from 0 to (L+63)/64 - 1: - tid goes from 0 to 63 - out_l = l_start + tid - For out_l < L, compute depthwise - But what about threads with out_l >= L? Actually, let me use `0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x)))` which is the tanh approximation of GELU. This is what PyTorch typically uses (the tanh approximation, not the exact erf). Wait, let me check: nn.GELU() in PyTorch defaults to `approximate='none'` which uses the exact erf-based formula. So I need to use the exact formula for correctness. The exact formula is: `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` CUDA has `erff` for float and `erf` for double. Let me use `erff`. Now for the kernel code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f)); } ``` Let me now write the complete kernel code. ```cuda __global__ void pointwise_depthwise_gelu_kernel( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pw, const float* __restrict__ b_pw, const float* __restrict__ W_dw, const float* __restrict__ b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (out_l < L) { float dw = 0.0f; const float* w_dw_ptr = W_dw + e * K; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw += y_pw_shared[tid + k] * w_dw_ptr[k]; } dw += b_dw[e]; y_act[b * E * L + e * L + out_l] = gelu(dw); } } __global__ void project_back_residual_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pb_T, // transposed, shape [E, C] const float* __restrict__ b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l = blockIdx.y; int c = threadIdx.x; if (c >= C) return; __shared__ float y_act_shared[512]; // Load y_act for this position into shared memory for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < E; i += blockDim.x) { y_act_shared[i] = y_act[b * E * L + i * L + l]; } __syncthreads(); float dot = 0.0f; const float* w_ptr = W_pb_T + c; #pragma unroll 8 for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { dot += y_act_shared[e] * w_ptr[e * C]; } int idx = b * C * L + c * L + l; out[idx] = dot + b_pb[c] + x[idx]; } ``` For kernel 1: - Grid: (B, E, (L + 63) / 64) - Block: 64 threads - Shared: 72 floats = 288 bytes For kernel 2: - Grid: (B, L, 1) - Block: 256 threads (but we only use 256, with C=256 it's exact) - Shared: 512 floats = 2KB Wait, for kernel 2, each block handles one (b, l) and all c=0..255. Actually, CUDA grid limits are typically 2^31-1 for x, 65535 for y and z. So grid.x=32, grid.y=4096, grid.z=8 is fine (< 65535). Let me now write the kernels: ```cuda __global__ void kernel1( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pw, const float* __restrict__ b_pw, const float* __restrict__ W_dw, const float* __restrict__ b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l = blockIdx.y; int e_tile = blockIdx.z * 64; int e = e_tile + threadIdx.x; if (e >= E) return; const int PAD = K / 2; // Compute pointwise for positions [l-PAD, l+PAD] float pw[9]; // K=9 #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) pw[i] = 0.0f; const float* w_pw_ptr = W_pw + e * C; #pragma unroll 4 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float w = w_pw_ptr[c]; int l_read = l - PAD; const float* x_ptr = x + b * C * L + c * L + l_read; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { if (l_read + i >= 0 && l_read + i < L) { pw[i] += w * x_ptr[i]; } } } float b_pw_val = b_pw[e]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { pw[i] += b_pw_val; } // Depthwise const float* w_dw_ptr = W_dw + e * K; float dw = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw += pw[k] * w_dw_ptr[k]; } dw += b_dw[e]; y_act[b * E * L + e * L + l] = gelu(dw); } ``` For this kernel: - Grid: (B, L, E/64) = (32, 4096, 8) - Block: 64 threads - Each thread handles one e - Registers: pw[9] = 9 floats, plus some temporaries. Should fit easily. - No shared memory needed! Memory access pattern: - x: each thread loads K=9 floats per c, for C=256 channels. Total 2304 loads per thread. But all 64 threads in a block load the same x values (for the same l). The GPU L1/L2 cache will make subsequent threads' loads hit cache. So effective reads from DRAM per block: ~2304 floats of x, amortized over 64 outputs. - W_pw: each thread loads C=256 floats. Adjacent threads load strided W_pw (e differs). These are L2 cache hits since W_pw is small. - W_dw: each thread loads K=9 floats. L2 cache hit. - y_act write: 64 writes, coalesced. Memory bandwidth per output: ~2304/64 + 256 + 9 + 1 ≈ 300 floats. For 67M outputs = 80GB. At 3.35 TB/s, ~24ms. Plus kernel overhead. Hmm, for the reference with cuDNN+ cuBLAS, the matmul-based approach would use Tensor Cores and be much faster. So this simple kernel might be slower. Let me reconsider. Maybe I should try to match cuDNN's approach by using Im2Col and then GEMM. Actually, PyTorch might already be doing this optimally. What if instead of 1 thread per e, I use 1 warp per (e, l) and have the 32 threads cooperatively load x and compute the dot product? This would be more like a reduction approach but with fewer redundant loads. Or what if I treat the pointwise as a matmul and use shared memory tiling? Let's think about kernel 1 as just pointwise first: - Input x: [B, C, L] - Output y_pw: [B, E, L] - This is equivalent to: y_pw[b, e, l] = sum_c x[b, c, l] * W_pw[e, c] + b_pw[e] - For fixed (b, l), this is a matvec: x[:, l] (size C) @ W_pw.T (size C x E) -> y_pw[:, l] (size E) - So for each (b, l), we have a [C] x [C, E] -> [E] matvec. With B=32, L=4096, we have 131072 independent matvecs, each of size [256] x [256, 512] = [512]. A matvec of [256] x [256, 512] = 131K multiply-adds. With 131072 matvecs, total = 17.2B ops. At 67 TFLOPS, ~0.25ms. Memory bandwidth isn't the bottleneck if we can use Tensor Cores. For a custom kernel to beat cuBLAS, we'd need to use Warp Matrix Multiply Accumulate (WMMA) or MMA instructions (Tensor Cores), which is quite complex. Alternatively, we can rely on the fact that PyTorch's Conv1d might not be using the most optimal path for this specific case, or that our fusion saves enough memory bandwidth to win despite not using Tensor Cores. But wait - for depthwise conv with groups=E, it's NOT a GEMM in the standard sense. Each channel is convolved independently. So cuDNN likely has a specialized path for this. But pointwise and project_back are standard GEMMs. If we use separate kernels calling into cuBLAS, we'd need to: 1. Pointwise: reshape x to [B*L, C], W_pw to [E, C], use SGEMM to get [B*L, E] 2. Depthwise: specific depthwise conv kernel (could use cuDNN or custom) 3. Project_back: SGEMM with [B*L, E] and W_pb But the task says "replace the forward computation ... with a custom CUDA kernel". Our ModelNew can contain the weights as nn.Conv1d and call custom kernels. We don't have to implement the matmuls ourselves. We can't beat that with custom matmuls. Or maybe we can just use a single custom kernel for depthwise+gelu+project_back+residual, while keeping pointwise as PyTorch. This reduces kernel launches and fuses some operations. Actually, let me re-read the task: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel." This implies the entire forward should be in custom kernels. Let me try yet another approach: use explicit shared memory for a tile-based matvec. For kernel 1 (pointwise), treating it as matvec per (b, l): - Block: 256 threads (8 warps) - Each block handles 1 (b, l) and computes all E outputs - Shared memory: x_shared[256] for x[:, l] - We load x into shared memory cooperatively - Then we can compute the matvec in various ways - Option A: Each thread computes some e outputs by dotting x_shared with W_pw[e, :] - Option B: Use warp-level reductions For 256 threads and E=512: - Each thread computes 2 e outputs - Thread i computes e=2i and e=2i+1 - For each e, dot = sum_c x_shared[c] * W_pw[e, c] - W_pw access: thread i loads W_pw[2i, c] and W_pw[2i+1, c] - With 256 threads, e ranges from 0 to 511 - W_pw is [512, 256]. Thread 0 accesses W_pw[0, c] and W_pw[1, c], thread 1 accesses W_pw[2, c] and W_pw[3, c], etc. - For a fixed c, threads load W_pw[:, c] with stride 2*256 = 512 floats. Strided! - But W_pw is small (512KB), so L2 cache hits. Alternatively, if each warp handles a contiguous range of e: - Warp 0: e=0..31, each of 32 threads handles one e - For fixed c, warp loads W_pw[0:31, c]. These are at offsets c, c+256, c+512, ..., c+31*256. Since cache lines are 128 floats, each load needs 32*256/128 = 64 cache lines. That's bad. Actually, W_pw is stored as [512, 256] in row-major. So W_pw[e, c] is at e*256 + c. For fixed c and varying e, accesses are strided by 256. For the dot product, as we iterate over c, we load W_pw[e, c] for all e and varying c. If we iterate c from 0 to 255, then for each c, we load W_pw[:, c] with stride 256. With 32 threads in a warp loading 32 different e values, that's 32 cache lines per c value. With 256 c values, that's 8192 cache lines. W_pw is only 512*256 floats = 131072 floats = 128K floats. At 128 floats per cache line, W_pw is 1024 cache lines. So we'd reload the same cache lines multiple times. In fact, W_pw is only 512KB. It fits entirely in L2 cache (50MB on H100). So all W_pw accesses should be L2 cache hits! The L2 cache hit latency is still a few cycles, but it's not going to DRAM. So the memory traffic for kernel 1 is dominated by x and y_pw. With caching of W_pw and W_dw, we just need to optimize x and y. For x at (b, l), it's 256 floats. Each block loads it once (via shared memory) = 256 floats. Then computes 512 outputs. y_pw writes = 512 floats. Total per block: 256 + 512 = 768 floats for 512 outputs. That's 1.5 floats per output. But wait, we need x for depthwise too. If kernel 1 is pointwise + depthwise + GELU, we don't write y_pw, we write y_act. But each output still needs x. For depthwise, each (b, e, l) needs x at l and 8 neighbors. So x loaded per output = 9 * 256 / output_count. If the block computes 512 outputs (all e at one l), it loads x at 9 positions = 2304 floats. Per output: 2304/512 = 4.5 floats. Plus y_act write = 1 float. Total ≈ 5.5 floats per output. For 67M outputs = 368M floats = 1.47GB. At 3.35TB/s, 0.44ms. Plus compute. Is that achievable? Let's see: 1.47GB at L2 cache rates (maybe not DRAM, but still memory bandwidth bound). The reference likely uses: - Pointwise SGEMM: [131072, 256] x [256, 512]. cuBLAS might achieve close to peak TFLOPS. - Depthwise: cuDNN optimized kernel. - Activation + project_back + residual: maybe fused or separate. For the pointwise SGEMM of size M=131072, N=512, K=256: - This is a fairly large GEMM. cuBLAS with Tensor Cores might achieve 1000+ TFLOPS in BF16 or ~500 TFLOPS in FP32. - Compute: 2*M*N*K = 2*131072*512*256 = 34.4B FLOPs - At 500 TFLOPS: 0.069ms - Memory for reading x (128MB) + W_pw (0.5MB) + writing y_pw (256MB) = 385MB. At 3.35TB/s: 0.115ms. Total reference could be in the 0.2-0.3ms range. For our kernel with 1.47GB of memory at 3.35TB/s: 0.44ms. That's likely slower than the reference. But wait! Our kernel might not actually read 9 * 256 floats per output from DRAM. It reads from L1/L2 cache if the same x has been loaded by another thread. Multiple blocks processing different e but the same (b, l) might share x through L2 cache. If we use grid (B, L, E_tile) with many blocks for the same (b, l), and they all load the same x values, the L2 cache might prevent redundant DRAM reads. But with 8 blocks per (b, l) all trying to load x simultaneously, they'd still need to read from DRAM at least once. Actually, if the 8 blocks are scheduled at different times, the later blocks would get L2 cache hits. But on an H100 with massive parallelism, they'd likely overlap. To load x only once per (b, l), we'd need all e channels to be computed in a single block. So block (b, l) with enough threads to compute all 512 e channels. If block has 512 threads, each thread computes one e. Then x is loaded once per block (either cooperatively into shared or via cache). This is much better. So let's try: - Grid: (B, L) = (32, 4096) = 131072 blocks - Block: 512 threads, thread e handles output channel e - Compute pointwise + depthwise + GELU for all e at (b, l) But wait, depthwise needs x at positions l-4..l+4. We have two options: 1. Each thread loads x at these 9 positions independently (2304 floats per thread, but shared via cache). 2. Use shared memory to load x once per block. With 512 threads, option 2: - Load x[b, 0:C, l-4:l+5] into shared memory: 256 * 9 = 2304 floats = 9KB. Easily fits. - Each thread computes pointwise and depthwise using shared memory. - Blocks = 131072 This is much better for memory bandwidth! Let me write this. ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pw, const float* __restrict__ b_pw, const float* __restrict__ W_dw, const float* __restrict__ b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l = blockIdx.y; int e = threadIdx.x; if (e >= E) return; const int PAD = K / 2; // 4 __shared__ float x_shared[256 * 9]; // 2304 floats = 9KB // Load x for positions [l-PAD, l+PAD] into shared memory int l_start = l - PAD; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < C * K; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / K; int k = idx % K; int read_l = l_start + k; if (read_l >= 0 && read_l < L) { x_shared[idx] = x[b * C * L + c * L + read_l]; } else { x_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute pointwise for K positions float pw[9]; const float* w_pw_ptr = W_pw + e * C; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 8 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { dot += x_shared[c * K + k] * w_pw_ptr[c]; } pw[k] = dot + b_pw[e]; } // Depthwise const float* w_dw_ptr = W_dw + e * K; float dw = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw += pw[k] * w_dw_ptr[k]; } dw += b_dw[e]; y_act[b * E * L + e * L + l] = gelu(dw); } ``` Wait, grid.y = L = 4096, which is less than 65535. Fine. Block = 512 threads. Shared = 2304 floats = 9216 bytes. Fits in 48KB. This kernel loads x[b, :, l-4:l+5] once per block (2304 floats from global). Then each of 512 threads computes its output using shared memory. Memory traffic: - x: 2304 floats per block, 131072 blocks = 302M floats = 1.2GB - W_pw: each block loads W_pw[0:511, 0:255] = 131K floats. But all blocks use the same W_pw regardless of (b, l). So W_pw stays in L2 cache. But with 131K blocks, total W_pw reads from L2 = 131K * 131K floats. However, since W_pw is only 512KB, it's fully cached and served from L2/constant memory at very high bandwidth. - W_dw: 512 * 9 = 4608 floats. Also fully cached. - y_act writes: 512 floats per block = 67M floats = 256MB. Total DRAM traffic: ~1.2GB (x reads) + 256MB (y_act writes) = ~1.5GB. For the reference, pointwise alone reads x (128MB) and writes y_pw (256MB) = 384MB. Plus depthwise reads y_pw and writes y_dw = 512MB. Plus project_back reads y_act and x, writes out = 640MB. Total ≈ 1.5GB. Plus weights. So our fused kernel for stage 1 might have similar memory traffic to the reference's separate stages. But stage 2 (project_back + residual) can save traffic. For kernel 2: - Grid: (B, L) = (32, 4096) - Block: C=256 threads - Loads y_act[b, 0:E, l] into shared: 512 floats = 2KB - Computes project_back for all c - Adds residual and writes out Memory traffic: - y_act reads: 512 floats per block = 256MB - x reads: 256 floats per block = 128MB (for residual) - out writes: 256 floats per block = 128MB - W_pb: cached Total: 512MB Reference for project_back + residual: reads y_act (256MB) + x (128MB) + writes out (128MB) = 512MB. Same. So our fused approach might not save memory bandwidth compared to a well-optimized reference that also fuses activation with project_back. But wait - our kernel 1 fuses pointwise + depthwise + GELU without intermediate writes. Reference likely does: pointwise -> write, depthwise -> write, activation -> write. That's 3 memory operations. Our kernel 1 does it in 1 pass. If reference doesn't fuse, it would have ~1GB of intermediate traffic. But modern cuDNN might fuse pointwise and depthwise, or depthwise and activation. Regardless, let me write this code and test it. The key advantage of our custom kernel is direct fusion. One concern: block with 512 threads. H100 has 2048 threads per SM, so each SM can run 4 blocks concurrently. With 131072 blocks and 132 SMs: 131072 / 132 = ~992 blocks per SM. At 4 concurrent, that's 248 waves. Fine. Occupancy with 512 threads per block: 4 blocks per SM = 2048 threads = full occupancy. Great! Now for kernel 2, block with 256 threads. Each SM can run 8 blocks = 2048 threads. Full occupancy. Let me write the complete code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f)); } __global__ void kernel1( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pw, const float* __restrict__ b_pw, const float* __restrict__ W_dw, const float* __restrict__ b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l = blockIdx.y; int e = threadIdx.x; if (e >= E) return; const int PAD = K / 2; extern __shared__ float x_shared[]; // Cooperative load of x for positions [l-PAD, l+PAD] int l_start = l - PAD; int total_x = C * K; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_x; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / K; int k = idx % K; int read_l = l_start + k; if (read_l >= 0 && read_l < L) { x_shared[idx] = x[b * C * L + c * L + read_l]; } else { x_shared[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute pointwise for K positions float pw[9]; const float* w_pw_ptr = W_pw + e * C; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 8 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { dot += x_shared[c * K + k] * w_pw_ptr[c]; } pw[k] = dot + b_pw[e]; } // Depthwise const float* w_dw_ptr = W_dw + e * K; float dw = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { dw += pw[k] * w_dw_ptr[k]; } dw += b_dw[e]; y_act[b * E * L + e * L + l] = gelu(dw); } __global__ void kernel2( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pb, const float* __restrict__ b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int l = blockIdx.y; int c = threadIdx.x; if (c >= C) return; __shared__ float y_act_shared[512]; // Load y_act for this position into shared memory for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < E; i += blockDim.x) { y_act_shared[i] = y_act[b * E * L + i * L + l]; } __syncthreads(); float dot = 0.0f; const float* w_ptr = W_pb + c * E; #pragma unroll 8 for (int e = 0; e < E; e++) { dot += y_act_shared[e] * w_ptr[e]; } int idx = b * C * L + c * L + l; out[idx] = dot + b_pb[c] + x[idx]; } ''' cpp_source = ''' void kernel1( const torch::Tensor& y_act, const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& W_pw, const torch::Tensor& b_pw, const torch::Tensor& W_dw, const torch::Tensor& b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K); void kernel2( const torch::Tensor& out, const torch::Tensor& y_act, const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& W_pb, const torch::Tensor& b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L); ''' ``` Wait, I need to check the function declarations. `load_inline` auto-generates pybind11 wrappers from the function names. Let me rewrite this properly. ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f)); } __global__ void kernel1_device( float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pw, const float* __restrict__ b_pw, const float* __restrict__ W_dw, const float* __restrict__ b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { // ... device code ... } __global__ void kernel2_device( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ y_act, const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W_pb, const float* __restrict__ b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { // ... device code ... } void kernel1( torch::Tensor y_act, torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor W_pw, torch::Tensor b_pw, torch::Tensor W_dw, torch::Tensor b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K) { dim3 grid(B, L); dim3 block(E); // 512 threads size_t shared_size = C * K * sizeof(float); kernel1_device<<<grid, block, shared_size>>>( y_act.data_ptr<float>(), x.data_ptr<float>(), W_pw.data_ptr<float>(), b_pw.data_ptr<float>(), W_dw.data_ptr<float>(), b_dw.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, E, L, K); } void kernel2( torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor y_act, torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor W_pb, torch::Tensor b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L) { dim3 grid(B, L); dim3 block(C); // 256 threads kernel2_device<<<grid, block>>>( out.data_ptr<float>(), y_act.data_ptr<float>(), x.data_ptr<float>(), W_pb.data_ptr<float>(), b_pb.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, E, L); } ``` And `cpp_sources` is just: ```cpp void kernel1(torch::Tensor y_act, torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor W_pw, torch::Tensor b_pw, torch::Tensor W_dw, torch::Tensor b_dw, int B, int C, int E, int L, int K); void kernel2(torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor y_act, torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor W_pb, torch::Tensor b_pb, int B, int C, int E, int L); ``` This should work. PyTorch's `nn.GELU()` with `approximate='none'` computes: `x * 0.5 * (1.0 + torch.erf(x / math.sqrt(2)))` Which is: `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` My CUDA implementation: `0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f))` This matches. So it uses the exact erf-based formula. Good. Let me also consider: is there a way to make the kernel faster by using float4 loads? For the shared memory load, the threads load x at strided positions. Using float4 for contiguous x values (same c, adjacent l) could help. Actually, for `x_shared,` each thread loads `idx = threadIdx.x + n * blockDim.x`. With blockDim.x = 512 and total_x = 2304, the loads per thread are at indices: threadIdx.x, threadIdx.x + 512, threadIdx.x + 1024, threadIdx.x + 1536, threadIdx.x + 2048. For thread 0: 0, 512, 1024, 1536, 2048. index 0 = c=0, k=0 index 512 = c=56, k=8 (approx) index 1024 = c=113, k=7 (approx) These are not contiguous in the original x tensor, but they are contiguous in x_shared. We write them to x_shared. The values we read from x are: x[b, 0, l-4] x[b, 56, l+4] x[b, 113, l+4]... Wait, x_shared is conceptualized as [C, K]. index = c*K + k. So: index 0 = c=0, k=0 -> x[0, l-4] index 1 = c=0, k=1 -> x[0, l-3] ... index 8 = c=0, k=8 -> x[0, l+4] index 9 = c=1, k=0 -> x[1, l-4] So adjacent k for the same c are adjacent in memory (since x[c, l] and x[c, l+1] are contiguous). So for thread 0 loading index 0, the next index it loads is 512 = c=56, k=8. But indices 0-8 for c=0 are contiguous in x memory! To improve coalescing, we could have each thread load a segment of contiguous memory. For example, each thread loads 4 or 8 contiguous elements. But since total_x = 2304 and blockDim.x = 512, most threads only load 4 or 5 elements. Actually, 2304 / 512 = 4.5. So each thread loads at most 5 elements. The loads are: - Thread t: indices t, t+512, t+1024, t+1536, t+2048 - These correspond to various (c, k) pairs scattered across x For better memory access, we could transpose the loop or assign threads differently. But with only 2304 floats per block and 512 threads, we end up with ~5 loads per thread. Since x is 128MB and L2 cache is 50MB, most of x might stay cached across blocks. And adjacent blocks process adjacent l values, which means adjacent blocks access adjacent x memory regions (x[:, l] and x[:, l+1]). So L2 cache should capture this temporal/spatial locality. I think the current design is reasonable. Let me compile and check correctness. One potential issue: register usage. Each thread in kernel 1 has: - pw[9] = 9 floats - Some temporaries (dot, w, etc.) - Should easily fit in 255 registers per thread. For kernel 2: - dot - y_act_shared is in shared memory, not registers - Should fit easily. Let me write the complete code.

<cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <math.h>\n\n__device__ __forceinline__ float gelu(float x) {\n return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118654752440f));\n}\n\n__global__ void kernel1_device(\n float* dtype=x.dtype)\n w_dw = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1).contiguous() # [E, K]\n b_dw = self.depthwise.bias.contiguous() if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.zeros(E, device=x.device, callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = '''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <math.h>\n\n__device__ __forceinline__ float gelu(float x) {\n const float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608028654f;\n const float coeff = 0.044715f;\n float x3 = x * x * x;\n return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + coeff * x3)));\n}\n\n__global__ void depthwise_gelu_kernel(\n float* __restrict__ y_act,\n const float* __restrict__ y_pw,\n const float* __restrict__ W_dw,\n const float* __restrict__ b_dw,\n int B, int E, int L, int K) {\n \n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int l = blockIdx.y;\n int e = threadIdx.x;\n \n if (e >= E) return;\n \n const int PAD = K / 2;\n float dw = 0.0f;\n \n const float* w_dw_ptr = W_dw + e * K;\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) {\n int read_l = l - PAD + k;\n float val = 0.0f;\n if (read_l >= 0 && read_l < L) {\n val = y_pw[b * E * L + e * L + read_l];\n }\n dw += val * w_dw_ptr[k];\n }\n dw += b_dw[e];\n \n y_act[b * E * L + e * L + l] = gelu(dw);\n}\n\nvoid dw_gelu(\n torch::Tensor y_act,\n torch::Tensor y_pw,\n torch::Tensor W_dw,\n torch::Tensor b_dw,\n int B, int E, int L, int K) {\n \n dim3 grid(B, L);\n dim3 block(E);\n \n depthwise_gelu_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(\n y_act.data_ptr<float>(),\n y_pw.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_dw.data_ptr<float>(),\n b_dw.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, E, L, K);\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = '''\nvoid dw_gelu(torch::Tensor y_act, torch::Tensor y_pw, torch::Tensor W_dw, torch::Tensor b_dw, int B, int E, int L, int K);\n'''\n\nfused_ops = load_inline(\n name=\"dw_gelu\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"dw_gelu\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\"],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(channels, expanded_channels, 1)\n self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, expanded_channels, kernel_size,\n padding=kernel_size // 2, groups=expanded_channels)\n self.activation = nn.GELU()\n self.project_back = nn.Conv1d(expanded_channels, channels, 1)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n residual = x\n x = self.pointwise(x)\n \n B, E, L = x.shape\n K = self.depthwise.kernel_size[0]\n \n w_dw = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1).contiguous() # [E, K]\n b_dw = self.depthwise.bias.contiguous() if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.zeros(E, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n \n y_act = torch.empty(B, E, L, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n fused_ops.dw_gelu(y_act, x, w_dw, b_dw, B, E, L, K)\n \n x = self.project_back(y_act)\n x = x + residual\n return x\n" }
